doc_id=740 igt_id=igt740-105 1910 1923 L B B M M M M M M B M M G T
language: Norwegian (nob)
line=1910 tag=L:         b. *En kjole, den trenger du ikke.
line=1911 tag=B:
line=1912 tag=B:
line=1913 tag=M:  28 Other views on the relation between topics and referential givenness have also been proposed. For instance,
line=1914 tag=M:  Reinhart (1981) claims that topics only need to be referential. Whether we take Gundel's claim as our point of
line=1915 tag=M:  departure, or Reinhart's, the prediction is the same regarding left-dislocation of bare singulars: If topics are
line=1916 tag=M:  assumed to correspond to token discourse referents, bare singulars are not expected to be topic expressions, since
line=1917 tag=M:  they are neither familiar, nor referential, on the token-level. Whether Gundel or Reinhart (or someone else) is
line=1918 tag=M:  right, is therefore not an issue in this section.
line=1919 tag=B:
line=1920 tag=M:                                                         46

line=4763 tag=B:
line=4764 tag=M:  56 The data that will be presented in this section represents a conservative view on what predicative position (or
line=4765 tag=M:  non-argument position) means, namely basically the position following the copular verb or copular particles.
line=4766 tag=M:  However, my claim that the predicative/non-predicative distinction is not a basic parameter for the use of
line=4767 tag=M:  Norwegian bare singulars holds for any definition of the predicative/non-predicative distinction, as long as it is
line=4768 tag=M:  assumed that there is one set of constraints that restricts the use of bare singulars in argument positions and
line=4769 tag=M:  another set of constraints (if mentioned at all) that restrict the use of bare singulars in predicative position. What
line=4770 tag=M:  this section does not concern, however, is the question of whether or not bare singulars are predicative in a
line=4771 tag=M:  semantic sense, meaning that they are semantically incorporated and/or unambiguously weak as in van
line=4772 tag=M:  Geenhoven, 1996, and Kallulli 1999, for instance. This question was partly considered in chapter 2, and will be

line=11361 tag=M:  103 An exclamation mark (!) in front of a phrase signals that this kind of phrase is usually unacceptable, but may
line=11362 tag=M:  be acceptable in very specific linguistic and non-linguistic contexts. The kind of phrase given in (8f) is only
line=11363 tag=M:  acceptable if it is modified by a relative clause and has an attributive (i.e. nonreferential) interpretation, as in Den
line=11364 tag=M:  mann som klarer denne oppgaven vil motta en premie ('that man who manages this task will receive an award'.)
line=11365 tag=M:  104 The inflectional paradigm for adjectives with respect to definiteness is often referred to as declension (see
line=11366 tag=M:  e.g. Neville, 2000, Netter, 1994), and adjectives are correspondingly marked as weak or strong. However, since
line=11367 tag=M:  all weak adjectives in Norwegian occur in definite nominals, whereas all strong adjectives occur in indefinite
line=11368 tag=M:  nominals, one might just as well (or even more successfully) refer to this paradigm as definiteness. Thus, I
line=11369 tag=M:  follow Vangsnes (1999) and refer to weak and strong adjectives as definite and indefinite ones, respectively.
line=11370 tag=M:  105 I assume with Gundel et al. (1993) that a determiner is definite if and only if it signals that its associated
line=11371 tag=M:  discourse referent is at least uniquely identifiable to the hearer, indefinite if it only signals that its referent is at
line=11372 tag=M:  least type identifiable (see section 2.9 for more details).
